B0000047CW|5.0|Its probably fair to say that this album, today, lies a bit in the shadow of the somewhat more well known and truly outstanding \\"Getz/Gilberto\\".  Don't let that fool you.  Jazz Samba is every bit as good.  As on Getz / Gilberto, Getz's playing here is gorgeous - the man had a one-of-a-kind tone on the tenor saxophone and it shines through here.  But as compared to Getz's other bossa nova efforts, including Getz / Gilberto, the guitar here is a bit more in the forefront - these pieces are really duets between Getz and Byrd on his guitar.  Byrd's guitar playing here is intricate and improvisational, and he and Getz are playing off each other.  On some of Getz's other bossa albums, the guitar playing is generally more subdued, a little more subtle and not as improvisational - perhaps more indicative of the \\"true\\" bossa style, as the guitarists on those albums were several of the Brazilians at the core of the bossa movement - but IMHO even if this CD is less authentic, the guitar playing is generally more effective here.  Another difference between this recording and Getz / Gilberto is the lack of vocals - the instruments take center stage on this album and they stay there.  Although I love the vocals on Getz / Gilberto (and on Getz's effort with Lois Bonfa, Jazz Samba Encore), there's something to be said for doing away with them and simply focusing on the music itself.     As to the tracks themselves, Desafinado (\\"Off key\\") was huge hit off the album.  The full album version is here of course, but a bonus track at the end of the disk is the abbreviated 45 rpm single version that was played on the radio at the time.  This track was also recorded for and included on Getz / Gilberto, but I prefer this version - here Getz's saxophone takes over the vocal role and in my estimation its more effective that way.  The Getz / Gilberto version sort of gets lost in the shuffle of the other excellent tracks on that disc.  Here, it stands out.    The other track that tends to get mentioned a lot is \\"Samba de uma Nota So\\" (\\"One Note Samba\\"), and also E Luxo So, but for me the real standout track on the album is Samba Triste (\\"Sad Samba\\"), a beautiful, plaintive, minor-key track that Getz absolutely soars on - I could put this track on single repeat and never get tired of it, and never reach for another CD again, ever.

0316017930|5.0|This book is both well-written and eye opening.  I began reading, \\"The Outliers,\\" on the recommendation of my younger adult son. He thought that as a psychologist and a parent, I'd find the premise, a critical analysis of what factors may account for extraordinary performances of contemporary as well as historical figures, quite engaging.  He was right.  Western culture seems so focused on the individual accomplishments of our \\"heroes\\" that we sometimes forget that many of those extraordinary accomplishments were the results of personal gifts like intellect, dedicated practice and tenacity but also, happenstance.  In the United States, in particular, we are closely wedded to the notion that success is inexorably tied to one's ability to pull one's self up by one's own bootstraps. Sometimes this perspective even prevents us for offering those in need, a helping hand believing that if such individuals or groups are truly motivated, they can help themselves out of their current or even historical quandary.  Having internalized this \\"bootstrap\\" notion, others sometime reject assistance believing that their accomplishments would be viewed as less \\"worthy\\" since the successes they sought were not then entirely the fruit of only their efforts.  Gladwell makes the case for our expanding our list of attributions for achievement beyond the boundaries of the individual to include all sorts of variables over which the individual had virtually no control.  These examples serve as useful reminders that we should admit to some questions of even of our most basic of assumptions.

0393338827|4.0|What would a massive financial crash be without Michael Lewis?  Having long ago cut open the sordid sardine can of Wall St. in his initial masterwork \\"Liar's Poker\\", he's demonstrated over and again, in works like \\"Moneyball\\" and \\"The Blind Side,\\" that his technique of transforming numbers into a compelling literary tool was no anomaly.  Here again, in \\"The Big Short,\\"  he ascends to the top of the crowded pile of recaps and rehashes of the 2008 debacle by offering up a compelling swirl of personalities, situations, and mathematical facts that reads more like a suspense novel than work of financial history, but for the knowledge we all have going in about where this story ends.     Unlike most of the rest of this quickly developing genre of \\"crisis lit\\", Lewis eschews a linear analysis of the mortgage crisis and its continuing aftershocks, choosing instead to take the reader on a visit inside the minds of three players who knew better, and emerged fabulously wealthy thanks to their perspicacity and guts.  Steve Eisman, Mike Burry, and the upstart team at Cornwall Capital, Jamie Mai and Charlie Ledley, all came into the mortgage market through different openings in the early 2000s.  Eisman was an old Wall St. hand and hedge fund manager, Burry an Asperger's suffer and cancer survivor who jettisoned his medical career in favor of the trader's life, and the Cornwall Capital boys were just a couple of guys with a Schwab account and an idea.  But each of these forces of financial nature saw, years before any of the statistical sophisticates on Wall St., that the subprime mortgage market was out of control, a fuse to a time bomb lit by the complex derivative contract known as a CDO.     Once armed with this insight these guys' jobs became startlingly simple: buy as many CDO's as institutions would sell them.  And institutions sold them like hotcakes, oblivious to the fact that they were insuring billions upon billions of loans with only the slimmest chance of repayment.The machine just wouldn't stop, since securitizing mortgages resulted in outlandish underwriting fees, and took risky loans off the books of the banks.  The idea was that the risk would be diversified into non-existence.  The reality was that unmeasured, untenable risk flowed into the veins of the financial system like poison.     That it would take minds that worked differently than anyone else to discern these facts isn't surprising.  What's surprising is that these three were apparently the only forces working to exploit what now seems obvious to everyone.  Lewis turns the spotlight on the herd mentality with ferocious effect here, planting seeds of distrust in almost any institutional actor in any sphere of society.  The little guy put his faith in the brilliance of the professionals' minds.  But the professionals didn't know what they were doing, or worse, just did it until they couldn't milk the dead cow anymore.     Lewis is helped greatly in the telling of this story by his background in bond finance during the 80's.  His mastery of his subject suffuses the book, but also makes one wonder where he was while the crisis was brewing.  But where was anyone besides Eisman, Burry, and Cornwall Capital?  By focusing on these three forces and revealing something about their reasoning and the workings of their minds, Lewis has given a valuable gift to those who might hestitate to question the logic of giants.

3899552512|4.0|this is adapted from a longer review at my blog liberal sprinkles      Paper comes to life in so many ways in this book. Art installations, portraits, animation characters, 3D posters, cutouts of figures, people, animals and objects big and small, Papercraft: Design and Art with Paper is full of fun examples of what can be crafted with paper.    There are numerous works I recognize, including some from Jen Stark and Ferris Staverman, whom I've blogged about. There are also pieces from other artists whose works I've seen on the Internet or pinned at Pinterest, so in some ways, this book was very familiar to me.    But there's a huge lot of other works that I was introduced to. A wide variety of works in multiple  structures and formats are presented. Some are functional, others purely decorative; some are playful, others thought-provoking. Nothing is boring or common. The works exude creativity - there's lots to grab your attention and give you ideas of how much can be created from a flat material.    My only complaint is there's very minimal information - if any at all - on the works and the artists. But there's still plenty of eye candy to enjoy.    4.5 stars

0767903862|5.0|The first time a read this book I enjoyed it. The second time I read it,  a couple of years later after learning a great deal more about Australia from other sources, I loved it. Bill Bryson does not take you to the typical tourist stops. Rather he takes you to many places best avoided, but he explains why in the process. His focus is on odd happenings in history, and quirky people he meets along the way. He is always researching museums and reading local papers to ferret out more little know factoids about the place. He does not spend a lick of time at the Great Barrier Reef except to tell us about the couple that was left there to try to snorkel thirty miles back to shore.  They were most likely eaten by sharks as they tried to reach a buoy resting on the other side of a deep channel where the big boys roam. Instead, he takes us to the distant shores of Western Australia, a place so vast that it has never been completely explored, to stare at blobs of matter called stromatolites credited with being the first bits of life in our universe. No matter where we are he always throws in a bit of sly, self-deprecating humor.  Fun read chock full of information and insights into the people and the place down under.

B005FUTBWS|4.0|I'm actually surprised that this is the first Kevin Smith I'm reviewing. I've really liked his films over the years, even MALLRATS, which Smith himself seems to dislike at times. Personally, I'd like to think that Kevin Smith is the John Hughes of my generation, but I grew up with Hughes the same as Smith did, so I'll just say that they're in the same league of filmmaking. However, I don't think John Hughes would have made a film quite like Red State and he would've stayed more in the comedy genre. With Red State, Kevin Smith broke away from the comedy genre and went into something completely different. Part psychological thriller, part social commentary, part horror film, part action flick; Red State is something uniquely it's own.    Red State is essentially about Abin Cooper (Michael Parks) and his Five-Point Trinity Church. Red State delivers a horrifying look at Christian fundamentalists and how absorbed into their own twisted views on Christianity they become. Basically, Kevin Smith portrays Abin Cooper as a cross between Fred Phelps and David Koresh.    The movie opens with three teenage boys being led to meet a 38 year old woman at her trailer in the middle of nowhere for casual sex. Not knowing it's a trap, they all are kidnapped and soon become the victims of the Five-Point Trinity Church and their plot to rid the world of sin and sinners.    Having been on the radar from the ATF for harboring illegal firearms, Abin Cooper is currently under surveillance so the ATF send in Agent Joseph Keenan (John Goodman)to head up the invasion on the Cooper compound. What happens next is essentially a stand-off between local law enforcement, headed up by sheriff Wynan (Stephen Root), and the ATF agents.    Basically that's the plot without spoilers and the best I can do to sum up the essentials in simplistic form. I'd rather not go further into details and spoil anything, so we'll leave it at that short synopsis. What I really enjoyed about the movie was how it shiftedgenres or combined several at once. Kevin Smith superbly weaved together a great narrative and character piece by combining different elements of filmmaking and the end result was Red State.    The acting in this film is truly top-notch and brilliant. Michael Parks totally embodies the Abin Cooper character and shines as the brilliant religious zealot. The sermon he delivers in the first act is truly creepy and so well acted that I was lost in every word he spoke. John Goodman, although looking kind of old and haggard, plays the conflicted ATF agent very well. If it weren't for Michael Parks stealing the show, I'd have a lot more to say about Goodman. He was excellently cast and really did a great job. However, every character was cast perfectly and acted their respective roles brilliantly.    The script was excellent. Smith has a knack for realistic and witty dialogue with a lot of banter between characters, but here he seems to transform that ability into monologues (the sermon Michael Parks delivers) and short interactions between characters. It's almost as if he streamlined what he normally does (long dialogue scenes between characters) and made the pacing faster and smoother. It works great here, but I doubt it would work as good in his previous films. I think that's what I admire most about Kevin Smith as a writer. He adapts to the film he's working on and writes what's best for the story. Here is no different.    The directing Kevin Smith did here is probably his best job yet. The camera work was so unlike any of his previous films, but yet it felt like a Kevin Smith film. The pacing was perfect and the film didn't stall out once. From the opening scene until the end credits, Smith has the viewer hooked and leaves them reeling at the end and wanting more. This is his best piece of filmmaking yet.    My gripes with the movie are really small. I felt the running time was a little on the short side and wanted to really get some back story and set-up with a few of the characters. I mean John Goodman comes into the film about thirty minutes in, and although he does a great job in this role, I felt cheated with his amount of screen time. The same can be said for Stephen Root and Kevin Pollack who are two amazing actors that I loved seeing, but they were hardly in the movie. This isn't saying this is bad, but I just wanted more. More characters, more story, and more time with the film. Clocking in at around 95 minutes, I felt this movie deserved more running time. I would really love to see a director's cut of the film and see another thirty minutes or so cut back into it. Overall, this gripe should say something for Kevin Smith and Red State. I wanted more and if that's my biggest gripe, that's saying something.    All in all I thought Red State was a great film. It made me run the gauntlet of emotions and I felt that I was truly watching something great. Mainly because I was doing just that. Red State is above average on all levels, and I think ultimately had it been longer and some of the plot points and characters developed more, it would be a near perfect film. My hat is off to Kevin Smith as he promoted this movie all on his own. He got it distributed himself and took it on tour like filmmakers used to do decades ago. That took serious balls to attempt, especially for this type of film. I won't go into too much detail here, so if you want to know how Red State came to be and how hard a time it had getting shown, look it up. It's just as interesting a story as the film is itself.    Overall, I recommend this movie highly, but warn that it should be watched with an open mind and not be taken seriously. Although, it should be taken serious enough when dealing with religious zealots like the Westboro Baptist Church and Fred Phelps. I'm glad Kevin Smith made this movie and I'm glad that the film sneers at people like Fred Phelps. Someone should show how nutty these people can be and how violent it can all end (ie- David Koresh). Red State has the balls to show them for who they are, even though it's fictionalized for entertainment sake.    Stephen J. Semones  Review originally hosted on The Back Lot  [...]

076115678X|2.0|Having read all of the reviews on this website, I bought the Curly Girl Handbook with high hopes. All I can say is, the method doesn't work for me. Perhaps it's because I've never attacked my hair with straighteners or hair-dryers in the past, or used a lot of products (I gave up trying to do anything with it at an early age), so maybe there just wasn't THAT much difference between a stretched-straight, blow-dried-to-death \\"before\\" / happy-ringletted \\"after\\" look for me - my hair has always been curly and in good condition.     The book basically advises you to clean your scalp with a bit of nasty-free conditioner (not shampoo); rinse; then condition your hair, leaving most of the conditioner in to hydrate the curls. And only to use your fingers to comb it. (There, that's it - saved you 188 pages of reading.) But instead of taming the frizz and giving me gorgeous locks, this method just made my hair go into greasy-looking coils - and that was on day 1, straight out of the shower! To have the hair of a dirty scumbag when you've just washed and dried the stuff is so disappointing. But maybe I'm just the exception to the rule - there seem to be a lot of people out there who swear by the CG method.     As for the book itself, I appreciate what it is trying to do, but wish it would treat its readers in a less patronising fashion. The non-stop puns, personal stories from random women about how growing up with curly hair has traumatised them for life (32 of them, most taking up a whole page each), \\"horoscope\\" chapter (because all girls love horoscopes, right?!?!?!?!?), \\"12-Step Recovery Program\\", and photos of the author's baby are all irritating space-fillers.     I did like the chapters on: why curly hair behaves in the way it does; homemade hair-care products (although these are all traditional recipes that you could easily find online); and the chapter showing you how to put your hair up. I am very grateful to the Curly Girl Handbook for opening my eyes to the grisly list of chemicals to be found in the average bottle of shampoo (how does the beauty industry get away with it?). But when you want a book that has almost everything Thai - this is the one.    The extensive introduction gives an excellent background on what you are eating and where is comes from.      This book meets my usual criteria for a good ethnic cook book:  1) It is written for the western reader.  Ingredients are identified by English names. Ingredients are possible to obtain - or reasonable substitutes suggested.   2) The writer has extensive in-country experience.  Thompson is Australian but spent years in Thailand.    3) I am leery of books written by professional cooks - which are often full of difficult restaurant recipes - good only when you have a staff to help.  This book is written for the home cook.  I have cooked a number of recipes from this book so far - without issues.   I should add that Thompson makes all his curry pastes from scratch.  The modern Thai cook does not do that - they buy from the tubs of fresh curry paste available at any market.  Unless you have loads of time and a very good Asian store nearby - you should too.  Thai curry paste in a number of different styles is available in many Asian markets - and Amazon - although more dear.  Take the recipe - and figure out which one he is making - and dip it out of the container.  (Full disclosure - I do a lot of ethnic cooking and not much phases me except too long ingredient lists and excessive preparation.)

While I understand that she wanted to let everyone understand what the characters are saying, that just doesn't seem realistic to me - why would anyone in real life speak like that? I think I would have preferred it if either the whole sentence had been in Spanish (you can probably get what they're talking about from the context even if you don't know any Spanish), or if she'd written the sentence entirely in English and then written something like \\"...,\\" Alex said in Spanish. Maybe it's just me, but Spanish used like it is in this book (and lots of other books) really annoys me.     Despite my dislike of the writing, Perfect Chemistry is a great romance novel with fully-developed characters. I'm planning on reading Rules of Attraction, the companion novel, soon - even though I'm not sure whether I'm going to enjoy it, since it sounds really similar to Perfect Chemistry...

0142410705|4.0|There is no doubt that John Green is an amazing writer - almost anyone who has read Looking for Alaska or Paper Towns would agree. What makes him so great, in my opinion, is that he manages to make the reader laugh out loud and cry within a few pages - sometimes even paragraphs. But his second novel, An Abundance of Katherines, has been critiqued several times for not showcasing this talent. Even though I understand where this critique is coming from (the book definitely showed John Green's distinct sense of humor, but didn't touch my heart or make me cry like his other novels), I don't think these reviews do the novel justice. It is, quite simply, a cute and entertaining read, which happens to be extremely well-written.     The characters' personalities were quirky, distinct, and fun to read about. To me, they are what make this story work, as the plot alone (which I have to admit was pretty predictable) would not have satisfied readers. The way he shows the readers his characters' personalities is what makes John Green's writing so special. I especially loved Lindsey and her relationship with Colin. One of my favorite parts of the novel were the cave scenes and the metaphor they provided (Lindsey's physical hiding-place being the only place she does not have to hide her true personality), which I thought was cleverly crafted.     The novel has been critiqued for not being as serious as Looking for Alaska or Paper Towns, but the summary alone shows the reader that this is supposed to be a light, funny, not completely realistic read. Especially the footnotes and the comments on the math show that the author doesn't take himself or his book too seriously.     One aspect of the novel I did have a problem with was the point-of-view. I thought the first-person narrator worked very well in the rest of John Green's writing, while the third-person narrator made this novel lack some of the teenage-authenticity I loved about his other novels (especially Looking for Alaska).However, maybe this sacrifice was necessary to help the author create a certain distance between the protagonist and himself - Colin's problem of taking himself too seriously wouldn't have fit John Green's self-deprecating style of writing.     Even though An Abundance of Katherines is not as emotional or powerful as John Green's other novels, I'd still recommend it if you're looking for a quick, funny and entertaining read. If you haven't read anything by this author yet, though, I'd suggest reading some of his other work first.

B004K4IZ3G|5.0|In the first \\"TRON\\", a video game designer named Kevin Flynn, searching for proof that his work was stolen from him, became trapped within the digital world he helped to create, forced to fight in gladiator-style games to the death against computer programs that appeared very human in the world of the Grid. It was a strange world in which the programs viewed the \\"Users\\" of the real world as godlike figures. TRON was the greatest gamer on the grid, and TRON fought for the Users.<br /><br />\\"TRON Legacy\\" takes us back to the world of the Grid, when Kevin's son, Flynn, follows a mysterious message from his long lost father and ends up trapped on the Grid himself. There, he finds things are not as wonderful as his father described since a troubled program called CLU began running things. Sam soon must go into hiding with his rediscovered dad and a lovely girl named Quorra who is more than she appears. Together, the three must plan a way to escape from the Grid and thwart CLU's dream of crossing through to the real-world and \\"perfecting\\" it. And what of TRON himself? Yes, he's in here too in a most surprising way.<br /><br />1982's TRON and 2010's TRON Legacy are now available in a variety of purchasing options. Myself, I picked up the Blu-Ray + DVD combo of each film separately. For me, this was the best way. I don't have use for digital copies, nor 3D discs, as much as I would have liked the collectible identity disc packaging, ha. I doubt it is even full-size anyway, but I'm not sure since I've never seen it in stores. Anyway, this review is for the Blu-Ray/DVD of the 2010 sequel to TRON (the Original Classic), TRON Legacy.<br /><br />As I said in the title of this review, this is a great sequel that surpasses the original in many ways. Not every way maybe, since one can't deny the first film's status as innovative, and groundbreaking, and all that jazz. It was a daring movie to make. Tron Legacy is a little safer, as sequels tend to be (though it's tough to really say that in this case, with TRON being a bit of an odd duck and nearly 30 years old). The story in TRON Legacy is simpler and easier to follow, I think, catering more to a wider audience, more than anything because it is less stiff and computer-geek-centric, I'd say. I imagine back when the first film came out, a lot of folks didn't know what all the computer jargon even meant. I mean, I know WE didn't have much hands on experience with computers back in `82 in MY parents' house. The outfits and light cycles and stuff still were cool through the eyes of a kid though. However, the original TRON, while I find it IS a very interesting movie with a great cast and good characters (though we only really got to know Flynn really well), is a film that one can, frankly, really fall asleep too. If you're determined to watch it, you may decide it's a pretty good movie. I think it is. But, no doubt, it takes concentration; absolutely no distractions. It's like... reading a book.  Miss a beat and you're lost. Allow yourself  to get lost, and you'll probably drift off to sleep. TRON Legacy is a different story. The visuals are far more real than the first film, which attempted to show off very primitive computer animation that wasn't actually realistic at all (but still kinda works in the world that film created). While TRON Legacy improves on character development as well as visuals, the plot is easier to understand with much livelier action, music, and fun. The emotional levels are much more present too. Yeah, you can nit-pick at some of the concepts in the film, but really, this is pure fantasy anyway, so if it's not interfering with your enjoyment and just adding to the story rather then mucking it up, why do that?  TRON Legacy is just lots of fun, full of visual eye-candy, and also loaded with lots of clever stuff for both new fans and fans of the original to appreciate. And, ya gotta love it when, nearly 30 years later, the original actors return to make a sequel probably noone in the general audience would have guessed would ever actually happen.  Yes, Jeff Bridges returns here as Kevin Flynn/Clu, Bruce Boxleitner returns as Alan Bradley/TRON,  and there is a franchise teaser on this DVD in which Dan Shor returns as RAM, in a sense, hinting that he and perhaps others may return for a third installment, which Bruce Boxleitner has already claimed to be a Go project. David Warner is suspect to be back the third time `round as well, which would be very cool, especially if Cindy Morgan is brought back as well, but I digress. Returning to the topic of Legacy, the are fresh faces on board too, including Olivia Wilde as Quorra, Garrett Hedlund as Sam Flynn, Michael Sheen, who is a highlight in the Bowie-esque roll of Castor/Zuse, Beau Garrett, another highlight in the drool-inducing roll of Gem, and Cillian Murphy doing a cameo as the son of David Warner's character, Dillinger, hinting that he will be back to play a villain in the third TRON film. Daft Punk, who provides the electronic soundtrack, also appear as D.J.s in the film.<br /><br />TRON Legacy is a great movie that pulls at your nostalgia-bone in particular if you have any fond memories of the 80's at all, or if you're a Disney fanatic like myself. All the stuff called back from the first film is just awesome, and that scene where Sam is checking out the arcade with Journey and the Eurythmics playing in the background is just too sweet. It's also just a great sci-fi flick with a great cast though. There is one thing I must mention, however, and I nearly forgot. There's been a lot of talk about the younger version of Kevin Flynn seen in the film. Basically, it's a CGI Jeff Bridges. Filmmakers have never learned that CGI isn't wholly perfect looking yet, especially with human characters, and it may never be. I don't have a big problem with CLU looking a little CGI since he's a digital entity anyway (though none of the others have that issue), but even I have to admit that they probably should have given a clear shot of CGI Flynn during the flashback scenes. The really clear, direct views didn't work that well. This is more a problem in the opening flashback than anywhere else. However, this does not spoil the movie for me. I hope it won't spoil this great movie for you. The Blu-Ray sadly has no commentary track, Disney's stuff rarely does, but it does have some nice behind the scenes featurettes, as well as a pretty cool Daft Punk music video with really retro TRON graphics and a cameo by Olivia Wilde, and you get the sneak peak at the upcoming TRON animated series, which is worth getting excited about with its great cast and cool visuals. \\"The Next Day: Flynn Lives Revealed\\" is probably the highlight bonus feature though, as it gives us a deeper look into what happened when Kevin Flynn disappeared and what happened when Sam returned. In other words, this is a cool set up for a 3rd film. I can't wait! As for the included DVD of the film, it holds the sneak peak at the animated series as well, plus two of the behind the scenes bonus features from the Blu-Ray. All in all, a nice package, but be sure to buy the original classic on Blu-ray too! In all honestly, the existence of TRON Legacy just makes the first one more interesting and easier to watch, because now you're thinking about how it led to the events of that film as well.ould have guessed would ever actually happen.  Yes, Jeff Bridges returns here as Kevin Flynn/Clu, Bruce Boxleitner returns as Alan Bradley/TRON,  and there is a franchise teaser on this DVD in which Dan Shor returns as RAM, in a sense, hinting that he and perhaps others may return for a third installment, which Bruce Boxleitner has already claimed to be a Go project. B004CAWKXE|3.0|I've heard people make the argument that MCR's previous albums all embrace different styles.  Why, then, should fans dislike a new album which similarly embraces a new style?    The ocean between Danger Days dwarfs the creek between Three Cheers and Black Parade.  Danger Days feels generic.  Gone is the edgy atmosphere that permeated both albums.  Instead, we get ballads, punk, and arena rock.  In fact, Danger Days reminds me of what happened to Linkin Park (regardless of what you think of them).  They forgot about the signature style that brought them to prominence and have subsequently lost any relevance.  The comparison is even more apt because both bands are known for emo-ish lyrics and a younger fanbase dealing with issues like break-ups,suicide, or both at once.  Music critics love to lampoon this sub-genre- perhaps both bands felt pressure and desire to step into more respectable musical climates.  Well, fine, sing about ponies if you want to, but don't abandon your amazing sound!    That's not to say all of the cuts are bad.  Na Na Na, Bulletproof Heart, and The Only Hope for Me Is You all feel like MCR- especially the former.  And the rest are ok- they might be good tracks on a more boring band's album.  U2 maybe?  But not for MCR.  What's worse, most of the tracks don't showcase Gerard Way's unique vocals.  His intonation is the lifeblood of the band, and the production of the album largely obscures it.    I know the album has largely been a success, but here's hoping MCR returns to more familiar territory in the future.

B004EPYZUI|5.0|I often read the reviews at Amazon and Netflix, but this is the very first time I've felt compelled to write one.  That's entirely due to this movie, every second of which I found to be mesmerizing and top-notch.  This is truly a fantasy masterpiece.    Based on the number of reviewers who, for reasons I'll never understand, seemingly didn't like it, I can't wholeheartedly recommend this movie to everyone.  Moreover, this movie would be unsettling for younger children, and I wouldn't recommend it for early teens either, due to its dark overtones.  But to me (and presumably others like me), this movie is a multiple orgasm of visual and auditory bliss, and an elevation of the action/fantasy genre that borrows the best of the elements in that genre and from them weaves together something unique.      It takes the slow-motion over-the-top fighting seqences from marshall arts films and the arresting visual graininess of movies like 300 and Sin City(without being overdone).  It is a marriage of photography and CGI ala Sky Captain and the World of Tomorrow (but not so animated as Beowulf).  It is a fantasy within a fantasy within a broken reality, reminiscint of the Matrix. Shutter Island, the Sixth Sense, and Inception.  But above all, it is its own, a consolidation and a permutation, raising the bar by culling the very best from what we have so far while infusing some new inspirations (and a whole lot of writer/director Zack Snyder).    The movie snob that lives inside my head compels me to point out that plot and storyline take a back seat to the audio and the video, existing primarily to provide a platform for the images and music, which perfectly compliment each other.  Don't get me wrong, there is a story here, it's just that the storytelling is not of the usual style.  It's a kind of a visual shorthand, done in such a way as to keep the viewer involved and the movie moving forward without getting in the way of the incredible imagery and dynamite, rocking soundtrack(which contains refreshing and inspired covers of some of the great rock tunes of yesteryear).  Like most action movies, the characters don't develop.  Instead, they're deftly executed snapshots of people, not simplistic but satisfying and sometimes self-mocking, done exactly as they should be done for this movie.        The movie makes a statement, perhaps several.  And it's all put together seamlessly, with ingenuity, taste, and a perfectly aggressive, non-stop pacing.  Unfortunately, it would appear that Sucker Punch and Zack Snyder deserve far more recognition than they're going to receive, but mark my words, this movie will continue to gain a following, and will go down in history as a mainstreamed cult classic in the same league as the Rocky Horror Picture Show and Firefly/Serenity.    If you kinda liked the movies I referenced above, you're gonna love this!

I implemented three different strategies described in the book in my own companies, and I am ecstatically pleased with the results. His ideas provide a highly effective way for any company--large or small--to identify new opportunities through applying the basic components of the scientific method. This allows a company to explore new opportunities in highly scaled ways and then to make reasoned assessments of their value. This is powerful stuff, as I have found out. One of the big secrets is to scale the opportunity, set up hypotheses regarding the change, and (best) develop a means of assessing the value and success of the opportunity. Each of his concepts are accompanied by detailed real-life examples. The book is well organized around Ries's key concepts and provide a very sensible approach that, if implemented accordingly, will result in success. Again, after applying several of the concepts myself, I appreciate the power of these approaches. They work. And I really like the way they work. He definitely is onto something here.    But the reader is left to figure out a lot of the details for implementing the concepts. The \\"how\\" of the approach is all but missing. As a result, the book has more the feeling perhaps of a series of general overview lectures that provide occasional nuggets that an attendee takes home. I want to see many more details (and perhaps fewer examples) of how a business person can set these ideas into motion. More, for example, like the approach used in \\"The Entrepreneurial Mindset.\\"     So I am left highly jazzed about the concepts presented here, but hungry for more detail as an actual user of the concepts. Regardless, the general approach he is advocating is square on the mark. They work and they are powerful.

B003GYEGNO|5.0|Tom Wolfe's books always impress me, and The Bonfire of the Vanities is definitely one of his best works, studying American culture and human behavior through the lens of a criminal trial in New York City.    Sherman McCoy is the main character of the book, but the book also follows the Bronx assistant District Attorney and a newspaper reporter, whose fates intertwine with Sherman's after he is entrenched in the court case. Interestingly, the only true protagonists in the book, the Lamb family, are the only ones who do not get much time on the page beyond the initial accident. They become mere pawns in a much bigger game of egos, where justice is served (or not served) only based on personal gain or loss. The true fate of the Lamb family is hardly relevant to anyone.    Wolfe brilliantly weaves an engaging story, and his prose are crystal clear and entertaining. The plot is enjoyable on the surface level, but the ramifications of everyone's actions will leave a reader thinking for days following the conclusion of this book. I definitely recommend this to anyone.

B0053Q9DHW|4.0|That's what I have after watching this dvd twice: a decidedly mixed reaction. Like many other reviewers, I'm a die-hard Jane Eyre fan, having read the book too many times to count and seen at least 6 film adaptations (I think there have been at least 8). This dvd is the 5th I've added to my collection, which my children find absolutely hilarious. But anyway, I wanted to see this one at the theater but it was only in my hometown for about a week, so I ordered it as soon as Amazon had it. I guess I should just lay out my pros and cons and let the chips fall where they may.<br /><br />Pros: The casting is possibly the best of any version I've seen. Mia Whatchamacallit (sorry, can't remember her name right now) was 21 when the film was shot, making her about the right age to play Jane. All the other versions I've seen cast actresses who were well past their teen years, so it was good to see a more-age-approriate Jane. She also did a nice job with the role, expressing shades of emotion in her face since her lines are frankly rather limited. Michael Fassbender is honestly too young (34) and too good-looking (he was the smokin' hot warrior Stelios in 300) but does a very good job inhabiting the character of Rochester. He's very gruff with just the right hint of humor to make Rochester the irritating but somehow intriguing guy he is. The other roles were also well-cast. Judi Dench is always amazing, and Jamie Bell (though not as handsome as St.John should be, imho) was appropriately stern. The cinematography of the movie is just lovely. I really liked the lighting. Everything at Gateshead is sunlit and colorful, belying the hellish existence Jane endures there. Lowood school is spare and colorless, and Thornfield is mostly lighted from underneath with the only light falling on the characters seeming to come from candles and fireplaces. This creates an air of mystery and confusion as the viewer can never really get a good enough view of the place to even be sure of its layout. I liked the notion of starting the story with Jane's escape from Thornfield and the rest being told in flashback, but I can't say if that would be confusing to someone unfamiliar with the story or not.<br /><br />Cons: I suppose nobody could condense this huge novel into a 2 hour movie and not leave out something I thought was important, but I felt too many details were omitted. Jane's character development as well as that of the relationship between Jane and Rochester were dealt with so quickly that they lost some of the depth of emotion they should have had. The conclusion of the film occurs so abruptly that I got a bit angry. It was sort of a \\"You've got to be kidding!\\" reaction that it just suddenly ended without what I thought was adequate closure. Some scenes, such as Jane's flight over the moors, were lovely but rather long, and I found myself wishing some of those scenes had been cut to give more scenes that \\"fleshed out\\" the story.<br /><br />So my mixed reaction still allows me to recommend the movie. I think it is a good and respectful adaptation of one of my favorite novels as well as a visually lovely film with a solid cast.ed the notion of starting the story with Jane's escape from Thornfield and the rest being told in flashback, but I can't say if that would be confusing to someone unfamiliar with the story or not. After having read many vampire stories it takes a lot to surprise me and to get me hooked on a story like that. While Marked has some of the typical YA boarding school clichÃ©s like the nice roommate, the pretty but mean blonde girl and the hot guy who - surprisingly - is the ex-boyfriend of said mean girl.    But overall the new approach of vampires could cancel out those clichÃ©s and made it more special. When you become a vampire, you aren't bitten but marked with a crescent moon on your forehead. To change into a vampire, you have to visit a school (House of Night) and the scariest thing is: Your body might reject the change and you might die.    I have to admit that the story started out a little bit slow, so in the end this novel really looks more like an introduction into this series. Most of the secondary characters are lacking some depth and are reduced to a few character traits, but I hope that this will change in the future novels.    Actually I have a similar \\"problem\\" with the love interest. While Erik is an incredibly hot guy and at first he appears like someone very likeable, I'm still not quite sure what to think about him. His interest in Zoey came quite fast and I couldn't really detect any development in their attraction. Therefore I'm curious to find out what will happen to them.    All in all, Marked was an enjoyable read and I'm looking forward to see how the storyline will develop. A lot of questions still need to be answered and I'm sure it could get even more interesting. Zoey is a likeable main character and I'm sure that I will like the next story told from her point of view as well.

I can't wait to read more novels by Maggie Stiefvater like The Scorpio Races, which will be released on October 18th.

B000001ELP|3.0|It's not surprising that this album was such a huge bomb.  Fans just weren't going to let Kiss be Pink Floyd, which is who they seemed to be trying to be with this one.  Having said that, it's not a bad record at all, and if you're the kind of fan who likes to cycle through a band's back catalog, this one is a nice diversion from their traditional \\"big dumb rock\\" (and I mean that in the best possible way) output.  The Bob Ezrin production is crisp and clean, particularly on the acoustic and orchestral material. The opening strums of \\"Just A Boy\\" still make my speakers sing nicely.    \\"A World Without Heroes\\" was the would-be hit single from this record, co-written by the great Lou Reed, and it should've been a bigger hit than it was.  A Gene Simmmons ballad that may have been a hit had it been given to Floyd or any of a number of more \\"respectable\\" bands, but just wasn't going to make a dent on the chart with the name \\"Kiss\\" attached to it. Elsewhere you find hard-rocking tunes like \\"The Oath\\", \\"I\\" (with the Gary Glitter backbeat that has served so many 80's hard rock acts well), and the instrumental \\"Escape From The Island\\".  The rest of the songs don't really stand on their own...they're connecting pieces for a nebulous storyline that's hard to make sense of through the lyrics, but then what great concept album isn't?  Mostly it's just hard to make sense of why a band like Kiss wanted to do an album like this, but I for one think they proved that they could do so beautifully.  It just will never be perceived that way, which is unfortunate.  Gene Simmons, in reviewing his band's records, said \\"As a Kiss album, I'd give it one star.  As a bad Genesis record, I'd give it two stars.\\"  It's actually much better than either of those, but he's got a point.  If they had written this piece and given it as a whole to a completely different band, maybe an up-and-coming Queensryche or Dream Theater, they might've made a very big name for themselves with it.  But Kiss is Kiss,and their fans weren't going to let them be anything else.  Simple as that. Maybe they should've done like what U2 did with \\"Passengers\\" and recorded it under a completely different name.   No one's gonna confuse \\"The Elder\\" (and for the record, putting that pretentious \\"Music From...\\" on the title didn't help matters any) with \\"Quadrophenia\\", but when you think of how many absolutely horrific concept albums have been attempted by bands over the years...I'm certainly not gonna lump this one in with those simply because it's got a KISS logo on the cover.  You could argue that it's a bit hackneyed, a bit contrived, a bit overdone (Paul could've definitely stood to dial back the \\"emoting\\" on the vocals on \\"Odyssey\\" - let's not tread on Meat Loaf's territory here, okay?), but the fact remains...it's a darn good record, no matter what Gene and Paul tell you.

B0000009S3|4.0|This almost-wholly instrumental album, released in 1969, is generally seen as the late Frank Zappa's magnum opus. `Hot Rats' marks the point where Zappa became serious about the music and left behind the quirky-spoken-narrative-embedded-in-the-song, the clowning and the theatrics. The result has survived the years well and still sounds good.<br /><br />Often the words `jazz' and `fusion' are used when discussing HR, but at heart it's rock music, with tight 4/4 rhythms broken by occasional syncopation and pauses to add interest. Extended complex instrumental pieces employ a range of instrumentation (like heavy use of saxophones for example, and the masterful violin of Jean-Luc Ponty) and the music makes the hips want to dance as much as it engages the cerebellum.<br /><br />Notable highlights for most listeners are the opener `Peaches en Regalia' with its immediately recognisable full-on melody dominated by brass over a heavy rhythm section, much sampled over the years and the ideal theme music; followed by `Willie the Pimp' with the incomparable Captain Beefheart growling out raucous, hilarious lyrics for a couple of verses followed by a 7-minute guitar solo by Frank over a heavy, insistent medium-tempo rhythm.<br /><br />The production was somewhat groundbreaking, in that Zappa used custom-built 16-track recording equipment and experimented a lot with overdubbing and tape speed changes. The finished product is a joy: musically very accomplished with tight compositions, masterly arrangements and definitely great driving music. However, serene and relaxing it ain't; so if it's ambient mood music you need, look elsewhere.<br /><br />Some disgruntlement exists amongst hardcore fans about the currently available CD version as against the original vinyl release. Zappa did the remix himself in the late 1980s, being a more than competent engineer, arranger and producer. The remixed CD is a different listening experience, no doubt about that: Frank considered it an improvement, whereas some fans don't. The ideal solution, of course, would be a two-CD offering in one package so the listener could compare, then choose. Are the original 1969 16-track masters still available? If so, how about it? This, for me, would earn five stars.The ideal solution, of course, would be a two-CD offering in one package so the listener could compare, then choose. Are the original 1969 16-track masters still available? If so, how about it? This, for me, would earn five stars.

0061714402|5.0|Dave Mustaine founder, guitarist and front man for the heavy metal band, Megadeth, typically generate a variety of emotions resulting from his stage banter, interviews and music.  Megadeth fans will instantly give the horns sign with one or both hands as they exhibit their sweet air guitar skills.<br /><br />Others who might be fans of a competing bands like, say, Mustaine's first band, Metallica, are likely to pontificate about the finer points of guitar skills between Mustaine and Metallica's Kirk Hammett.  And, still others who are aware of Mustaine's conversion to Christianity are keenly interested in the strength or weakness of his walk of faith.<br /><br />As an avid student of celebrities and personalities as well as a Christian, I was eager to dig into the newly released paperback edition of Dave's autobiography, Mustaine: A Heavy Metal Memoir.  I'll be honest: I wasn't expecting much from the book because, well, I've never read a head banger's autobiography before and really thought I was going to be bored out of my aging mind.  Boy, was I wrong.<br /><br />The book is very heavy in personal introspection and transparency.  The reader will learn very quickly what make Mustaine tick and what ticks him off.  Megadeth and Metallica fans will read lots of inside scoop on the history of both bands and lots of stories behind the songs.  Dave also shares a lot of the interpersonal dynamics between him and the various configurations of his heavy metal enterprise.<br /><br />Christians who pick up the book expecting to read something along the lines of B.J. Thomas's Home Where I Belong will be shocked.  The language in the book is raw.  Very raw.  And, while I'm not one who uses the same kind of language as Mustaine chooses to use, I found his transparency and his simple, level-headed approach to his faith actually quite refreshing.<br /><br />For those of you who run, not walk, from all things Christian, don't be such a wuss.  Mustaine's book isn't \\"churchy\\".  Actually, it's far from it.  He states his faith in a matter-of-fact manner and doesn't try to jam anything down the reader's throat. B002MQYOFW|5.0|I put off reading this book for a long time. From the description, I just didn't think I would like it. Post-apocalyptic stories unnerve me (and not in a good way), so I just ignored it. Finally, I got tired of hearing all my friends and co-workers gush about it and not understanding what they were talking about, so I decided I would start it and see how it went.    I began the book at 7pm last night, and I finished it this morning at 2am. It was THAT good. I literally could not put it down (I even read while I was eating dinner...no easy task when you're eating spaghetti). I very rarely think that a book deserves 5 stars, but this one definitely does.    It is disturbing of course. We're talking about 12-18 year-olds killing each other in mostly hand-to-hand combat. But, the violence is not the center feature here, which keeps it from being too dark. The story, as told through the eyes of 16 year old Katniss, is nothing short of touching and honestly beautiful.    One thing I really liked about the book is that it doesn't read like a period piece. A lot of stories set after the apocalypse or after the fall of America begin to sound like a story that could have just as easily been set in the Dark Ages. It isn't so here. This is truly a unique world where although the nation as we know it today is no more, remnants of it still remain. Although Katniss, as a citizen of the poor District 12, has to hunt for her own food and live in poverty, they own a tv. They occasionally have electricity. The Hunger Games are basically a reality show that every citizen of Panem is required to watch. The citizens of the wealthy Capitol are almost exclusively vain, with crazy fashions and plastic surgery is the norm (sound familiar?). This gives the story such a unique twist that it sets it apart from others.    The story also gives you a lot to think about: the path our own society is taking, the limits of our humanity, the things we take for granted, etc. BUT, Collins finds a way totell this story and make you think about things in such a way that it doesn't come off as preachy or like she has an agenda.    I'm just really impressed and recommend it to anyone. I'm about halfway through the second book now, and it just keeps getting better.

0634023020|5.0|I just received my copy a few days ago, and I was impressed after listening to all of the recordings first on the accompanying CD. Here is an excerpt from the introduction:<br /><br />\\"On earlier projects, I would write out the entire set of arrangements and then find myself needing to rewrite a lot of the music because I couldn't keep from veering off the roadmap into improvisation-land. So with this project, I just wrote little notes to myself on a lead sheet, did some reharmonization, and tried to improvise as much as possible on the whole thing. Then I went back and transcribed the rest of it. It was a bit grueling and a lot more work, but I've never felt more satisfied with a book project, and I truly feel fortunate to have the opportunity to share it.\\"<br /><br />Even the author said it was a grueling process, and his efforts do show themselves in this body of work. The arrangements do have the improvised feel to them, but there is cohesion between the chord melody work and single line runs. The pieces can get spatial, with a lot of single-line breaks. That might not be what some fingerstyle players want, who are partial to playing consistent syncopation. This is a book of chord-melody arrangments, not Travis-style fingerpicking tunes. For the experienced jazzer, these tunes should be a fun undertaking to learn, with elements you will likely use in your own material.<br /><br />These pieces are for advanced level guitarists. For the newbie to solo jazz guitar arrangements, this will be more challenging without strong technique and feel, and sight-reading skills. There is TAB under the notation, but that only shows you where the fingers go. Of course, the CD can help you a lot with learning how to execute the tunes. Like many jazz arrangements, you don't have to play it exactly how it's written. That all depends on your experience and jazz theory knowledge. The book contains arrangments of tunes that Miles made famous, not just his compositions.<br /><br />Song list:<br /><br />All Blues<br />All of You<br />Blue in Green<br />Bye Bye Blackbird<br />Four<br />Freddie Freeloader<br />I Could Write a Book<br />Milestones<br />Nardis<br />Nefertiti<br />Seven Steps to Heaven<br />Solar<br />So What<br />There is No Greater Love<br />When I Fall in Love<br /><br />Just listen to the CD alone and you will want to make this an addition to your set. He mostly plays a nylon string guitar and regular acoustic guitar on most of the tracks, along with an older-sounding archtop on at least one tune. The sound quality coming from his nylon and acoustic guitars is great.<br /><br />If you want challenging, modern chord-melody arrangements of tunes that Miles made popular, get this book. B002VH7P4O|4.0|Given the notable number of comments regarding stem breakage in this Beatles USB, I was perhaps fortunate in finding a used example in like-new condition with intact stem.  The stem certainly seems fragile and I am always slightly nervous each time I handle the USB.  Hence, I quickly copied both the Flac and MP3 files to my PC and repacked the USB carefully in its liner and box.  I am happy that EMI made the copy-and-paste of the music files easy and uncomplicated with no need of music file registration, in stark contrast to Apple iTunes files.  The fragile stem is worrisome, as its breakage renders removal of the USB from its apple-shaped stand/case almost impossible, although another reviewer suggested the clever idea of using super glue-type adhesive to reattach the stem.  Nonetheless, EMI should have made the stem much more robust and resistant to breakage, especially for such a pricey and limited-production item.      In contrast, the apple-shaped stand-case is remarkably heavy, solid, and strong--it could easily serve as a projectile or rock-like weapon.  The engraving of the band's name in the stand-case lends the uSB an elegant aura;    it appears classy and cool sitting on a desk or shelf.  This underscores the impressive 14.9GB capacity of the tiny USB, of which only 9.21GB is occupied, leaving 5.69GB potentially for the buyer to add additional files.    Unfortunately the empty space is not readily accessible for adding files by the buyer and the USB's brief instructions and read-me files do not reveal how to do so, although, based on some reviewer suggestions, I surmise this can be done using somewhat esoteric--at least to a PC-ignorant user like I--algorithms.      However, I certainly agree with those who worry that making the space easily accessible to the lay buyer would create the potential for erasing mishaps; inadvertent erasure of any of the Beatles music files would be tragic,   especially for an expensive and limited-run music USB.  I suggest the considerable unused space would be much better used for additional Beatles recordings, such as the three 2-CD \\"Anthology\\" sets, \\"Let It Be...Naked\\", \\"Live   at the BBC\\", and perhaps even \\"Live at the Hollywood Bowl\\"; the latter has yet to be issued on CD and, despite its debatable musical value, would have been a fitting addition for a limited-edition set such as this USB.  Ideally, this USB should have included the \\"Mono\\" box set in Flac and MP3, but it needs more than the remaining 5.69GB.  However, the apple-shaped stand-case seems large enough to easily accomodate two USBs; the 2nd USB could accomodate the \\"Mono\\" box set.   The addition of the \\"Mono\\" box set, plus the other Beatles recordings I mentioned above (\\"Let It Be...Naked\\", \\"Live at the BBC\\", the three 2-CD \\"Anothology\\" sets, \\"Live at the Hollywood Bowl\\") would have greatly increased the value-for-money quotient of the USB.  As I am writing this, I realize the remaining 5.69GB of the single USB may not have accomodated all of the non-\\"Mono\\" recordings I suggested, but all of the recordings I suggested should fit on 2 USBs.    The Beatles USB is an elegantly compact presentation of the Beatles official stereo recordings that is very user-friendly for both the Flac and MP3 music files, and nicely graces any shelf or desk it sits upon, despite its high price (which may be commensurate with its limited production run).  Its value-for-money--and perhaps desirability and uniqueness, especially with inclusion of \\"Live at the Hollywood Bowl\\"--would have been much higher if the \\"Mono\\" box set and the other recordings cited above had been included with expansion of the set to two USBs.
B002VPE1AW|4.0|The script of Avatar is an obvious re-telling of the 1904 novel *Green Mansions.*  Maybe Freud was right all along:  There is no originality.  All the elements of the movie are in the novel:  the remnants of a mysterious race of people living in a remote forest where they are threatened by scoundrels trying to exploit the resources of the planet by destroying the ecosystem which is represented by a gigantic root system supporting a huge tree which gets destroyed at the end.      Unfortunately, the emotional impact of the novel does not exist in Avatar. The author of *Green Mansions,* William H. Hudson, made use of a highly effective device to convey his passionate love of nature which was represented by a beautiful seventeen-year-old girl who was probably the last surviving member of her race. Here is how Hudson presented that passion through the words of the story's hero:    \\"Why, I began to ask myself, was Rima so much to me?  It was easy to answer that question:    Because nothing so exquisite had ever been created.  All the separate and fragmentary beauty and melody and graceful motion found scattered throughout nature were concentrated and harmoniously combined in her. How various, how luminous, how divine she was! A being for the mind to marvel at, to admire continually, finding some new grace and charm every hour, every moment, to add to the old.  And there was, besides, the fascinating mystery surrounding her origin to arouse and keep my interest in her continually active.    That was the easy answer I returned to the question I had asked myself.  But I knew that there was another answer--a reason more powerful than the first.  And I could no longer thrust it back, or hide its shining face with the dull, leaden mask of mere intellectual curiosity.  Because *I loved her;* loved her as I had never loved before, never could love any other being, with a passion which had caught something of her own brilliance and intensity, making a former passion look dim and commonplace in comparison--a feeling known to everyone, something old and worn out, weariness even to think of. \\"      The love story as presented in Avatar was pretty lame compared to that I think.  I wonder if anyone involved in the film genuinely felt the distress of the original author over the impending loss of the rain forest.

B0054N73EY|4.0|Five years after releasing their Grammy-winning 2006 effort [[ASIN:B000EMGAOY Stadium Arcadium]], the Red Hot Chili Peppers return with their tenth studio effort, [[ASIN:B0054N73EY I'm With You]].  A lot happens during a five year hiatus, most notably the fact that guitarist John Frusciante left the band in 2009. Luckily, the band has been known to have a revolving door of guitarists and guitarist Josh Klinghoffer fits right into the mold.  The question most fans had for the band's new effort aside from their personnel change was what would the sound be after a five-year break. Unfortunate for the band, enjoyable first single \\"The Adventures of Rain Dance Maggie\\" did little to highlight what the new album might sound like.  Overall, the band sounds solid, even if I'm With You is by no means a valedictory effort.<br /><br />The effort opens mysteriously and somewhat off-putting with \\"Monarchy of Roses,\\" most certainly a stark contrast to previous opener \\"Dani California.\\" that said, after the track evolves, it turns out to be quite enjoyable and creative on the band's part.  It doesn't take long for Kiedis to shine vocally or for Flea's mad bass skills to shine through.  Follow up cut \\"Factory of Faith\\" is more down the earth, if slightly less brilliant than it's predecessor.  The cut opens with Flea's distinct bass and finds frontman Kiedis 'talking' through the verse as opposed to singing in a vocal manner. By The refrain finds Kiedis belting and the band as a whole yields some clever musical ideas, particularly newbie Klinghoffer.  \\"Brendan's Death Song\\" may be lengthy clocking in just twenty seconds shy of six minutes, but it may be the album's valedictory moment. The buildup of the cut is notable as is the well-penned refrain which Kiedis sings so passionately: \\"Like I said/you know I'm almost dead/You know I'm almost gone/And when the drummer drums/He's gonna play my song/to carry me alone...\\" Overall, a stunning cut.<br /><br />\\"Ethiopia\\" is another enjoyable cut, most notable for the funky groove and consummate musicianship (Klinghoffer's final chord on guitar is a brilliant musical touch).  The drum mix on \\"Annie Wants a Baby\\" is particularly noteworthy, accentuating Chad Smith's percussive touch and skill.  The unpredictability of the harmonic progression and Klinghoffer's guitar riffs are highlights of this 'A-' cut.  \\"Look Around\\" is solid and finds Kiedis talking through the verses while overtly singing on the refrain.  The instrumental bridge provides a nice change of pace and scope.<br /><br />Single \\"The Adventures of Rain Dance Maggie\\" lays well, even if it is a bit too similar in scope to Stadium Arcadium's better promo single \\"Dani California.\\" The refrain is catchy enough, though it is the bluesy guitar riffs and buttressing bass lines that solidify this cut.  \\"Did I Let You Know\\" is as funky as anything else featuring a great trumpet solo by Mark Bulger.  \\"Goodbye Hooray\\" sounds the slightest bit saturated in sounds as there is 'a lot to take in.' That said, the sounds are appealing despite nearly edging towards the cacophonous. \\"Happiness Loves Company\\" proves to be a welcome change of pace given its pianistic opening and catchy refrain.<br /><br />\\"Police like is enjoyable, particularly because of its easygoing sound, but its expansive five and a half minute length diminishes its likability.  \\"Even You Brutus?\\" atones through its stark contrast to anything else on the LP.  Kiedis's vocal performance is manic and Klinghoffer's guitar parts are killer. Penultimate cut \\"Meet Me At the Corner\\" is superb showcasing the band in a lighter sound.  Closer \\"Dance, Dance, Dance\\" is a bit clumsy, but ok.  At this point, the band have more than presented enough goods.<br /><br />Overall, [[ASIN:B0054N73EY I'm With You]] is another solid addition to the band's discography and to the rock catalogue. For the most part it is an enjoyable set, even where it falls short of being revolutionary. No, \\"Dani California\\" or \\"TellMe Baby\\" are hard to come by here, but there are plenty of capable cuts to please hardcore and casual fans alike.but there are plenty of capable cuts to please hardcore and casual fans alike.

There's also excellent liner notes that discuss the album.<br /><br />Both versions are worthwhile and, given that the remaster doubles the length of the album AND has a previously unreleased song recorded for the sessions, gives a slight edge to the remaster however I still like the sound of the first edition more.<br /><br />If you could afford both it would be worthwhile picking up both for their respective merits but either way this is an essential album.<br /><br />Incidentally there's a 21st unlisted bonus track a work out of \\"MacBeth/Birds\\" that runs about 5 minutes. 0670033375|3.0|The first chapter is rather long and thin on details relevant to the thesis of the book. There are some details about the environmental problems from mining and logging in Montana. This seems useful for understanding how societies collapse. But then Diamond spends a lot of pages on droll problems facing residents in a small valley in Montana. There are sanctimonious passages about how new-comers to the state from California are driving up real-estate prices, buying up the land and living isolated in gated communities. These problems are already covered in popular culture to the point of being cliche. The author recalls how he was horrified at  a movie theater placed near some farmland. He blames this on right-wing extremists that are opposed to government zoning laws. He has a point, but this is a bit of a red herring. He never explains how this is relevant to the rest of his book. It seems that this first chapter is a bit of soap-box for Diamond to talk about anecdotes from his vacation town. I was really disappointed to read this chapter as it is a bizarre way to start a book that is supposed to be an anatomy of why societies rise and fall. While the topics he brings up are important and need more awareness, they have little bearing on the overarching theme of the book and I am surprised that the editors allowed him to place this navel-gazing chapter to start the book.        The next few chapters are a bit better. Diamond gives backgrounds to a few remote islands that had societies that collapsed. These are stone-age societies living on the fringes of life and death. There is a good overview of the problems they faced and the likely causes for their demise. In this respect Jared Diamond's writing is superb. Here is an author who is gifted in research and presenting the facts or at least some well-founded speculation. But I found these societies to be outliers on the spectrum of humanity and only marginally instructive on problems faced in modern society.Diamond explores a few other early historic societies then jumps to the age of the Vikings. The omission of covering the collapse of the ancient Greeks or Roman empire is quite surprising. Throughout the book Diamond is using collapses of other societies to teach us about the problems facing our society. Surely at least one chapter covering opinions on the fall of the Roman empire would be material to the ideas of this book. Instead we get a detailed, and at times a bit long and boring, description of the problems facing remote settlements on Greenland.           As I continued reading this book I got the impression that this book is less a study of collapses and more of an opportunity for the author to travel to exotic places around the world. Throughout the book the author goes on tangents that are unrelated to the thesis of his book. There is the chapter of indignant Montana country folks indignant at the rich Californians coming to their quaint little valley, then there is a chapter dedicated to environmental problems in modern China or another about soil erosion in Australia. These are probably pretty important issues in their own right, but they are not important in the context of this book.          Diamond's writing can be really great. I loved Guns Germs & Steel, but this follow-up does not live up to that standard. It seems like Diamond started this book with a different thesis and maybe the editors or publisher changed the title to make it more catchy. I am not going to recommend this book to people who liked Guns, Germs and Steel. This books is pretty lengthy, and I think there are many other books that have more force and cohesion.

B0033AI4CK|5.0|\\"I must make my witness.\\" --An important line of dialogue, said by Howard Beale (portrayed by Peter Finch, in an Oscar-winning role) from a film that remains ever prophetic, ever realistic, ever relevant, ever prescient.    I make no bones about it; I love this movie and it is my favourite of all-time. I first saw it in about 1998 or so, and I've been in love and enraptured by the film ever since. It hasn't lost any of its power; as America, and the world, struggle once more with a sluggish economy, feelings of personal and political impotence (related to unnecessary foreign entanglements!), it is important to remember that above all else, we, as citizens of a great nation, need to act. An important part of the famous \\"Mad as Hell\\" monologue from the film:     \\"We know things are bad - worse than bad. They're crazy. It's like everything everywhere is going crazy, so we don't go out anymore. We sit in the house, and slowly the world we are living in is getting smaller, and all we say is, 'Please, at least leave us alone in our living rooms. Let me have my toaster and my TV and my steel-belted radials and I won't say anything. Just leave us alone.'\\" And that is why action, now more than ever, is important.     It is rather troubling and disturbing to me to me that so many people, who actually were alive during the era that this movie was released, are unaware of it. A very, very culturally and historically significant film. It has been regarded rather highly by many critics, both then and now. The only reason I can find behind this ignorance stems from the fact that this is a largely pessimistic film and an open call to arms --two things that Americans during the bicentennial (and even now) did not want to hear. Thus, it is not surprisingly that the Best Picture winner that year (in a contest that included not just Network, but also Taxi Driver, All the President's Men, and Bound for Glory), that the winner was Rocky. It was also a sign of the times that even though Rocky won --by far the most optimistic bunch of the movies up for the award--it still was fairly a downer (Rocky, after all, did lose the boxing match).     It is a sad commentary that this call to arms has not been heeded; it seems (based on voting numbers and statistics) that Americans gave up on America when it was obvious the Great Society of LBJ had failed them. Again, it may be the method of delivery; nobody wants to be reminded of how bad things are and  that they may (or may not) be complicit.     I speak so much of politics in this review because, for better or for worse, this is a political film, from a highly political era.     The direction is superb and Network is definitely one of the better films from its director, Sidney Lumet, who died earlier this year. Admittedly, there are some fairly weak works in his cannon, but that is bound to happen, as he was active from 1957 to 2007, a remarkable stretch of time; however, if an active artist only produces 1 good work in 50 years, that is enough (some only produce 1 or 2, period!). Luckily, this is not the only masterpiece in Lumet's cannon, which includes 12 Angry Men (1957) and Dog Day Afternoon (1975).    The writing is also top-notch, from the very political and polemical Paddy Chayefsky. His great works include Marty (1955) and The Hospital (1971) --both of which, in addition to Network, yielded awards for Best Screenplay.    And, of course, the acting is also well-regarded. It won 3 of the 4 acting awards (only losing Best Supporting Actor). It is a pity that Peter Finch won posthumously, that Bill Holden died shortly after, and that Faye Dunaway's work slipped in quality thereafter (Mommie Dearest, anyone?). It seems to be one of those great moments in time, when quality actors, a seasoned director, and a well-recognized writer came together to produce a work far greater than the sum of its parts.    There are, however, some glaring flaws.     Among them: the cinematography. I don't think it matters if you actually did see this on the Big Screen or at home; as important as images are, they don't seem to be what matters. No one, that I know of, has ever commented on the quality of the actual images.     And, of course, there are the several, abrupt tonal shifts. The movie has been, and somewhat accurately, noted for slipping into some sort of farcical self-parody at some point. It does start out excellent, drag a bit in the middle, and finish on an absurd sort of note. How much of this was intentional, I do not know.     There are, of course, some dated elements. These sort of things are inescapable in a satire such as this, unfortunately. Fortunately, the film does transcend these shortcomings, for the most part.     This is overall an excellent film, well worth the watch even if it does have some flaws. Network stands proudly as part of America's rich cultural legacy. The themes it touches upon have not gone away; indeed, in the era of the 24 hour news cycle, the film rings even truer.
